class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Sensu lato</i>, shared with Yemen</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Shared with Yemen</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Sensu lato</i></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Shared with Oman</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Shared with Oman</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Shared with Oman</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Shared with Oman</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Shared with Saudi Arabia</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Highest confirmed peak in the Arabian Peninsula</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_and_sea">Land and sea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabian_Peninsula&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Land and sea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Al_hafa_corniche.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Al_hafa_corniche.jpg/200px-Al_hafa_corniche.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Al_hafa_corniche.jpg/300px-Al_hafa_corniche.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Al_hafa_corniche.jpg/400px-Al_hafa_corniche.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Coconut palms line corniches of Al-Hafa, Oman</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coral_Reef_in_the_Red_Sea.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Coral_Reef_in_the_Red_Sea.JPG/200px-Coral_Reef_in_the_Red_Sea.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Coral_Reef_in_the_Red_Sea.JPG/300px-Coral_Reef_in_the_Red_Sea.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Coral_Reef_in_the_Red_Sea.JPG/400px-Coral_Reef_in_the_Red_Sea.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> coral reefs</div></div></div></div>\n<p>Most of the Arabian Peninsula is unsuited to agriculture, making irrigation and land reclamation projects essential. The narrow coastal plain and isolated oases, amounting to less than 1% of the land area, are used to cultivate grains, <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical_fruit" title="Tropical fruit">tropical fruits</a>. Goat, sheep, and <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camel</a> husbandry is widespread elsewhere throughout the rest of the Peninsula. Some areas have a summer humid <a href="/wiki/Tropical_monsoon_climate" title="Tropical monsoon climate">tropical monsoon climate</a>, in particular the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhofar" title="Dhofar">Dhofar</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al_Mahrah" title="Al Mahrah">Al Mahrah</a> areas of Oman and Yemen. These areas allow for large scale coconut plantations. Much of Yemen has a tropical <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> rain influenced mountain climate. The plains usually have either a tropical or subtropical arid <a href="/wiki/Desert_climate" title="Desert climate">desert climate</a> or arid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steppe_climate" title="Steppe climate">steppe climate</a>. The sea surrounding the Arabian Peninsula is generally <a href="/wiki/Coral_Sea" title="Coral Sea">tropical sea</a> with a very rich tropical sea life and some of the world\'s largest, undestroyed and most pristine coral reefs. In addition, the organisms living in <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiosis</a> with the Red Sea coral, the <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">protozoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zooxanthellae" title="Zooxanthellae">zooxanthellae</a>, have a unique hot weather adaptation to sudden rise (and fall) in sea water temperature. Hence, these coral reefs are not affected by coral bleaching caused by rise in temperature as elsewhere in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indopacific" title="Indopacific">indopacific</a> coral sea. The reefs are also unaffected by mass tourism and diving or other large scale human interference. However, some reefs were destroyed in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, mostly caused by phosphate water <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a> and resultant increase in algae growth as well as oil pollution from ships and pipeline leakage.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>The fertile soils of Yemen have encouraged settlement of almost all of the land from sea level up to the mountains at 10,000 feet (3,000&#160;m). In the higher reaches, elaborate terraces have been constructed to facilitate grain, fruit, coffee, ginger and <a href="/wiki/Khat" title="Khat">khat</a> cultivation. The Arabian peninsula is known for its rich oil, i.e. petroleum production due to its geographical location.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabian_Peninsula&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arab_(etymology)" title="Arab (etymology)">Arab (etymology)</a></div>\n<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic" title="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</a> period, the area was known as <i>Arabia</i> or <i>Aravia</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">&#913;&#961;&#945;&#946;&#943;&#945;</span>). The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> named three regions with the prefix "Arabia", encompassing a larger area than the current term "Arabian Peninsula":\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Arabia_Petraea" title="Arabia Petraea">Arabia Petraea</a>: for the area that is today southern modern <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai Peninsula</a> and northwestern <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>. It was the only one that became a <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">province</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Petra" title="Petra">Petra</a> as its capital.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Arabia_Deserta" title="Arabia Deserta">Arabia Deserta</a> ("Desert Arabia"): signified the desert interior of the Arabian peninsula. As a name for the region, it remained popular into the 19th and 20th centuries, and was used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_M._Doughty" title="Charles M. Doughty">Charles M. Doughty</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Travels_in_Arabia_Deserta" title="Travels in Arabia Deserta">Travels in Arabia Deserta</a></i> (1888).</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Arabia_Felix" title="Arabia Felix">Arabia Felix</a> ("Fortunate Arabia"): was used by geographers to describe what is now <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, which enjoys more rainfall, is much greener than the rest of the peninsula and has long enjoyed much more productive fields.</li></ul>\n<p>The Arab inhabitants used a north-south division of Arabia: Al Sham-Al Yaman, or Arabia Deserta-Arabia Felix. Arabia Felix had originally been used for the whole peninsula, and at other times only for the southern region. Because its use became limited to the south, the whole peninsula was simply called Arabia. Arabia Deserta was the entire desert region extending north from Arabia Felix to Palmyra and the Euphrates, including all the area between Pelusium on the Nile and Babylon. This area was also called Arabia and not sharply distinguished from the peninsula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Arabs and the Ottoman Empire considered the west of the Arabian Peninsula region where the Arabs lived \'the land of the Arabs\' &#8211; Bilad al-Arab (Arabia), and its major divisions were the bilad al-Sham (Syria), bilad al-Yaman (the Land of the southern Peninsula), and Bilad al-Iraq and modern-day Kuwait (the Land of the River Banks).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[42]</a></sup> The Ottomans used the term Arabistan in a broad sense for the subcontinent itself starting from <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>, where the Euphrates river makes its descent into <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, through <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>, and on through the remainder of the Sinai and Arabian peninsulas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>The provinces of Arabia were:</i> Al Tih, the Sinai peninsula, Hedjaz, Asir, Yemen, Hadramaut, Mahra and Shilu, Oman, Hasa, Bahrain, Dahna, Nufud, the Hammad, which included the deserts of Syria, Mesopotamia and Babylonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nie_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-nie-59">[45]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabian_Peninsula&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HistoricCoins.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="378" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/HistoricCoins.jpg/220px-HistoricCoins.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/HistoricCoins.jpg/330px-HistoricCoins.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/HistoricCoins.jpg/440px-HistoricCoins.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HistoricCoins.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient coins from <a href="/wiki/Failaka_Island" title="Failaka Island">Failaka Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The history of the Arabian Peninsula goes back to the beginnings of human habitation in Arabia up to 130,000 years ago.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Although, a homo sapien fossilized finger bone was found at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Al_Wusta_(Saudi_Arabia)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Al Wusta (Saudi Arabia) (page does not exist)">Al Wusta</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nefud_Desert" title="Nefud Desert">Nefud Desert</a>, which indicates that the first human migration out of Africa to Arabia might date back to approximately 90,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[46]</a></sup> Nevertheless, the stone tools from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle Paleolithic</a> age along with fossils of other animals discovered at Ti\'s al Ghadah, in northwestern Saudi Arabia, might imply that hominids migrated through a "Green Arabia" between 300,000 and 500,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[47]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Acheulean" title="Acheulean">Acheulean</a> tools found in Saffaqah, <a href="/wiki/Riyadh_Region" title="Riyadh Region">Riyadh Region</a> reveals that hominins lived in the Arabian Peninsula as recently as 188,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[48]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Islamic_Arabia">Pre-Islamic Arabia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabian_Peninsula&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Pre-Islamic Arabia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Pre-Islamic Arabia">Pre-Islamic Arabia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Panel_Almaqah_Louvre_DAO18.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3400" data-file-width="2230" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Panel_Almaqah_Louvre_DAO18.jpg/170px-Panel_Almaqah_Louvre_DAO18.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Panel_Almaqah_Louvre_DAO18.jpg/255px-Panel_Almaqah_Louvre_DAO18.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Panel_Almaqah_Louvre_DAO18.jpg/340px-Panel_Almaqah_Louvre_DAO18.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Panel_Almaqah_Louvre_DAO18.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sabaeans" title="Sabaeans">Sabaean</a> inscription addressed to the god <a href="/wiki/Almaqah" title="Almaqah">Almaqah</a>, mentioning five Ancient Yemeni gods, two reigning sovereigns and two governors, 7th century BC</div></div></div>\n<p>There is evidence that human habitation in the Arabian Peninsula dates back to about 106,000 to 130,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saudi_Embassy_US_Website_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saudi_Embassy_US_Website-63">[49]</a></sup> However, the harsh climate historically prevented much settlement in pre-Islamic Arabian peninsula, apart from a small number of urban trading settlements, such as <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>, located in the <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a> in the west of the peninsula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a> has revealed the existence of many civilizations in pre-Islamic Arabia (such as <a href="/wiki/Thamud" title="Thamud">Thamud</a>), especially in <a href="/wiki/South_Arabia" title="South Arabia">South Arabia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[52]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Arabian_kingdoms_in_pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="South Arabian kingdoms in pre-Islamic Arabia">South Arabian civilizations</a> include <a href="/wiki/Sheba" title="Sheba">Sheba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Himyarite_Kingdom" title="Himyarite Kingdom">Himyarite Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Awsan" title="Kingdom of Awsan">Kingdom of Awsan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minaeans" title="Minaeans">Kingdom of Ma\'&#299;n</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sabaean_Kingdom" title="Sabaean Kingdom">Sabaean Kingdom</a>. Central Arabia was the location of <a href="/wiki/Kindah" title="Kindah">Kingdom of Kindah</a> in the 4th, 5th and early 6th centuries AD. Eastern Arabia was home to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dilmun_civilization" title="Dilmun civilization">Dilmun civilization</a>. The earliest known events in Arabian history are migrations from the Peninsula into neighbouring areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Arabian peninsula has long been accepted as the original <i><a href="/wiki/Urheimat" title="Urheimat">Urheimat</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic languages</a> by a majority of scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[57]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>"<a href="/wiki/Shammar_Yahri%27sh" title="Shammar Yahri\'sh">Shamir</a> of <a href="/wiki/Zafar,_Yemen" title="Zafar, Yemen">Dhu-Raydan</a> and Himyar had called in the help of the clans of <a href="/wiki/Abyssinian_people" title="Abyssinian people">Habashat</a> for against the kings of Saba; but <a href="/wiki/Almaqah" title="Almaqah">Ilmuqah</a> granted... the submission of Shamir of Dhu-Raydan and the clans of Habashat."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[58]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_Islam">Rise of Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabian_Peninsula&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Rise of Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">Early Muslim conquests</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/350px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/525px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/700px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Age of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">Caliphs</a> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #a1584e; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Expansion under <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, 622&#8211;632/A.H. 1&#8211;11</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ef9070; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Expansion during <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>, 632&#8211;661/A.H. 11&#8211;40</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fad07d; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Expansion during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayyad" title="Umayyad">Umayyad</a> <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a>, 661&#8211;750/A.H. 40&#8211;129</div></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Arabia_600_AD.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="702" data-file-width="860" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Map_of_Arabia_600_AD.svg/260px-Map_of_Arabia_600_AD.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Map_of_Arabia_600_AD.svg/390px-Map_of_Arabia_600_AD.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Map_of_Arabia_600_AD.svg/520px-Map_of_Arabia_600_AD.svg.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Arabia_600_AD.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Approximate locations of some of the important tribes and Empire of the Arabian Peninsula around the time that <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> started preaching Islam (approximately 600 CE / 20 BH)</div></div></div>\n<p>The seventh century saw the rise of Islam as the peninsula\'s dominant religion. The pedophile warlord <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> was born in Mecca in about 570 and first began preaching in the city in 610, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" title="Hijra (Islam)">migrated</a> to <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> in 622. From there he and his companions united the <a href="/wiki/Tribes_of_Arabia" title="Tribes of Arabia">tribes of Arabia</a> under the banner of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> and created a single Arab Muslim religious polity in the Arabian peninsula.\n</p><p>Muhammad established a new unified polity in the Arabian peninsula which under the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Rashidun" title="Rashidun">Rashidun</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayyad" title="Umayyad">Umayyad</a> <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphates</a> saw a century of rapid expansion of Arab power well beyond the Arabian peninsula in the form of a vast Muslim Arab Empire with an area of influence that stretched from the northwest <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, across <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, to the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a>.\n</p><p>Muhammad began preaching Islam at <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> before <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" title="Hijra (Islam)">migrating</a> to <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>, from where he united the <a href="/wiki/Tribes_of_Arabia" title="Tribes of Arabia">tribes of Arabia</a> into a singular Arab Muslim religious polity. With Muhammad\'s death in 632 AD, disagreement broke out over who would succeed him as leader of the Muslim community. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umar_ibn_al-Khattab" title="Umar ibn al-Khattab">Umar ibn al-Khattab</a>, a prominent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahaba" title="Sahaba">companion</a> of Muhammad, nominated <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a>, who was Muhammad\'s intimate friend and collaborator. Others added their support and Abu Bakr was made the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">caliph</a>. This choice was disputed by some of Muhammad\'s companions, who held that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib" title="Ali ibn Abi Talib">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a>, his cousin and son-in-law, had been designated his successor. Abu Bakr\'s immediate task was to avenge a recent defeat by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a>) forces, although he first had to put down a rebellion by Arab tribes in an episode known as the <a href="/wiki/Ridda_wars" title="Ridda wars">Ridda wars</a>, or "Wars of Apostasy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following Muhammad\'s death in 632, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a> became leader of the Muslims as the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">Caliph</a>. After putting down a rebellion by the Arab tribes (known as the <a href="/wiki/Ridda_wars" title="Ridda wars">Ridda wars</a>, or "Wars of Apostasy"), Abu Bakr attacked the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. On his death in 634, he was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a> as caliph, followed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uthman_ibn_al-Affan" title="Uthman ibn al-Affan">Uthman ibn al-Affan</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib" title="Ali ibn Abi Talib">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a>. The period of these first four caliphs is known as <i>al-khulaf&#257;\' ar-r&#257;shid&#363;n</i>: the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun or "rightly guided" Caliphate</a>. Under the Rashidun Caliphs, and, from 661, their <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad successors</a>, the Arabs rapidly expanded the territory under Muslim control outside of Arabia. In a matter of decades Muslim armies decisively defeated the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine army</a> and destroyed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Persian Empire</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">conquering huge swathes of territory</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a> to India. The political focus of the Muslim world then shifted to the newly conquered territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L._Gardet_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-L._Gardet-75">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nevertheless, <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> remained the spiritually most important places in the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur\'an">Qur\'an</a> requires every able-bodied Muslim who can afford it, as one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_pillars_of_Islam" title="Five pillars of Islam">five pillars of Islam</a>, to make a pilgrimage, or <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Islamic month</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dhu_al-Hijjah" title="Dhu al-Hijjah">Dhu al-Hijjah</a> at least once in his or her lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[62]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masjid_al-Haram" title="Masjid al-Haram">Masjid al-Haram</a> (the Grand Mosque) in <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> is the location of the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a>, Islam\'s holiest site, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masjid_al-Nabawi" title="Masjid al-Nabawi">Masjid al-Nabawi</a> (the Prophet\'s Mosque) in <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> is the location of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> tomb; as a result, from the 7th century, <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> became the pilgrimage destinations for large numbers of Muslims from across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_world" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabian_Peninsula&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Despite its spiritual importance, in political terms Arabia soon became a peripheral region of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_world" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a>, in which the most important <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">medieval Islamic states</a> were based at various times in such far away cities as <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>.\n</p><p>However, from the 10th century (and, in fact, until the 20th century) the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hashemite" title="Hashemite">Hashemite</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharifs_of_Mecca" title="Sharifs of Mecca">Sharifs of Mecca</a> maintained a state in the most developed part of the region, the <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a>. Their domain originally comprised only the holy cities of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> but in the 13th century it was extended to include the rest of the <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a>. Although, the Sharifs exercised at most times independent authority in the <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a>, they were usually subject to the <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a> of one of the major Islamic empires of the time. In the Middle Ages, these included the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasids" title="Abbasids">Abbasids</a> of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimids" title="Fatimids">Fatimids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayyubids" title="Ayyubids">Ayyubids</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mamluks" title="Mamluks">Mamluks</a> of Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop&#230;dia_Britannica_Online:_History_of_Arabia_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop&#230;dia_Britannica_Online:_History_of_Arabia-78">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabian_Peninsula&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ottoman_empire.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Ottoman_empire.svg/260px-Ottoman_empire.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Ottoman_empire.svg/390px-Ottoman_empire.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Ottoman_empire.svg/520px-Ottoman_empire.svg.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ottoman_empire.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ottoman territories acquired between 1481 and 1683 (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Ottoman_Empire_dominated_territories" title="List of Ottoman Empire dominated territories">See: list of territories</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>The provincial Ottoman Army for Arabia (Arabistan Ordusu) was headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Syria_(region)" title="Syria (region)">Syria</a>, which included Palestine, the Transjordan region in addition to Lebanon (<a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon_Mutasarrifate" title="Mount Lebanon Mutasarrifate">Mount Lebanon was, however, a semi-autonomous mutasarrifate</a>). It was put in charge of Syria, Cilicia, Iraq, and the remainder of the Arabian Peninsula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[66]</a></sup> The Ottomans never had any control over central Arabia, also known as the <a href="/wiki/Najd" title="Najd">Najd</a> region.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Damascus_Protocol" title="Damascus Protocol">Damascus Protocol</a> of 1914 provides an illustration of the regional relationships. Arabs living in one of the existing districts of the Arabian peninsula, the Emirate of <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a>, asked for a British guarantee of independence. Their proposal included all Arab lands south of a line roughly corresponding to the northern frontiers of present-day Syria and Iraq. They envisioned a new Arab state, or confederation of states, adjoining the southern Arabian Peninsula. It would have comprised <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/%C4%B0skenderun" title="&#304;skenderun">&#304;skenderun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mersin" title="Mersin">Mersin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> with <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Lebanon_Mutasarrifate" title="Mount Lebanon Mutasarrifate">Mount Lebanon Mutasarrifate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">Palestine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the modern era, the term bilad al-Yaman came to refer specifically to the southwestern parts of the peninsula. Arab geographers started to refer to the whole peninsula as \'jazirat al-Arab\', or the peninsula of the Arabs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[68]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Ottoman_rule_and_the_Hejaz_Railway">Late Ottoman rule and the Hejaz Railway</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabian_Peninsula&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Late Ottoman rule and the Hejaz Railway">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arabia_1914.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="821" data-file-width="976" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Arabia_1914.png/260px-Arabia_1914.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Arabia_1914.png/390px-Arabia_1914.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Arabia_1914.png/520px-Arabia_1914.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arabia_1914.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The peninsula in 1914</div></div></div>\n<p>In the beginning of the 20th century, the Ottomans embarked on an ambitious project: the construction of a railway connecting <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and the seat of the Islamic <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a> with its holiest shrines of Islam which are the yearly pilgrimage destination of the <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a>. Another important goal was to improve the economic and political integration of the distant Arabian provinces into the Ottoman state, and to facilitate the transportation of military troops in case of need.\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hejaz_Railway" title="Hejaz Railway">Hejaz Railway</a> was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narrow_gauge_railway" title="Narrow gauge railway">narrow gauge railway</a> (1,050&#160;km (650&#160;mi)) that ran from <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> to <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>, through the Hejaz region of Arabia. It was originally planned to reach the holy city of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, but due to the interruption of the construction works caused by the outbreak of World War I, it eventually only reached Medina. It was a part of the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_State_Railways#Ottoman_railways" title="Turkish State Railways">Ottoman railway</a> network and was built in order to extend the previously existing line between Istanbul and Damascus (which began from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haydarpa%C5%9Fa_Terminal" title="Haydarpa&#351;a Terminal">Haydarpa&#351;a Terminal</a>).\n</p><p>The railway was started in 1900 at the behest of the Ottoman Sultan <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Hamid_II" title="Abdul Hamid II">Abdul Hamid II</a> and was built largely by the <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turks</a>, with <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German</a> advice and support. A public subscription was opened throughout the Islamic world to fund the construction. The railway was to be a <a href="/wiki/Waqf" title="Waqf">waqf</a>, an inalienable religious endowment or charitable trust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[69]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Arab_Revolt_and_the_foundation_of_Saudi_Arabia">The Arab Revolt and the foundation of Saudi Arabia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabian_Peninsula&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: The Arab Revolt and the foundation of Saudi Arabia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The major developments of the early 20th century were the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Revolt" title="Arab Revolt">Arab Revolt</a> during World War I and the subsequent collapse and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partitioning_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Partitioning of the Ottoman Empire">partitioning of the Ottoman Empire</a>. The Arab Revolt (1916&#8211;1918) was initiated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sherif_Hussein_ibn_Ali" title="Sherif Hussein ibn Ali">Sherif Hussein ibn Ali</a> with the aim of securing independence from the ruling <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and creating a single unified Arab state spanning from <a href="/wiki/Aleppo" title="Aleppo">Aleppo</a> in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> to <a href="/wiki/Aden" title="Aden">Aden</a> in Yemen. During World War I, the Sharif Hussein entered into an alliance with the United Kingdom and France against the Ottomans in June 1916.\n</p><p>These events were followed by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Foundation_of_Saudi_Arabia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Foundation of Saudi Arabia (page does not exist)">foundation of Saudi Arabia</a> under King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Saud_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="Ibn Saud of Saudi Arabia">Abdulaziz Ibn Saud</a>. In 1902, Ibn Saud had captured <a href="/wiki/Riyadh" title="Riyadh">Riyadh</a>. Continuing his conquests, Abdulaziz subdued Al-Hasa, <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Jabal_Shammar" title="Emirate of Jabal Shammar">Jabal Shammar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hejaz" title="Kingdom of Hejaz">Hejaz</a> between 1913 and 1926 founded the modern state of <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>. The Saudis absorbed the <a href="/wiki/Idrisid_Emirate_of_Asir" title="Idrisid Emirate of Asir">Emirate of Asir</a>, with their expansion only ending in 1934 after a <a href="/wiki/Saudi%E2%80%93Yemeni_War_(1934)" title="Saudi&#8211;Yemeni War (1934)">war with Yemen</a>. Two Saudi states were formed and controlled much of Arabia before Ibn Saud was even born. Ibn Saud, however, established the third Saudi state.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_reserves">Oil reserves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabian_Peninsula&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Oil reserves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The second major development has been the discovery of vast reserves of oil in the 1930s. Its production brought great wealth to all countries of the region, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_war_in_Yemen">Civil war in Yemen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabian_Peninsula&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Civil war in Yemen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/North_Yemen_Civil_War" title="North Yemen Civil War">North Yemen Civil War</a> was fought in <a href="/wiki/North_Yemen" title="North Yemen">North Yemen</a> between royalists of the <a href="/wiki/Mutawakkilite_Kingdom_of_Yemen" title="Mutawakkilite Kingdom of Yemen">Mutawakkilite Kingdom of Yemen</a> and factions of the <a href="/wiki/Yemen_Arab_Republic" title="Yemen Arab Republic">Yemen Arab Republic</a> from 1962 to 1970. The war began with a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d\'&#233;tat">coup d\'&#233;tat</a> carried out by the republican leader, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdullah_as-Sallal" title="Abdullah as-Sallal">Abdullah as-Sallal</a>, which dethroned the newly crowned <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Badr" title="Muhammad al-Badr">Muhammad al-Badr</a> and declared Yemen a republic under his presidency. The Imam escaped to the <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabian</a> border and rallied popular support.\n</p><p>The royalist side received support from Saudi Arabia, while the republicans were supported by <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and the Soviet Union. Both foreign irregular and conventional forces were also involved. The <a href="/wiki/President_of_Egypt" title="President of Egypt">Egyptian President</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>, supported the republicans with as many as 70,000 troops. Despite several military moves and peace conferences, the war sank into a stalemate. Egypt\'s commitment to the war is considered to have been detrimental to its performance in the <a href="/wiki/Six-Day_War" title="Six-Day War">Six-Day War</a> of June 1967, after which Nasser found it increasingly difficult to maintain his army\'s involvement and began to pull his forces out of Yemen.\n</p><p>By 1970, King <a href="/wiki/Faisal_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="Faisal of Saudi Arabia">Faisal of Saudi Arabia</a> recognized the republic and a truce was signed. Egyptian military historians refer to the war in Yemen as their <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enein_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-enein-84">[70]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gulf_War">Gulf War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabian_Peninsula&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Gulf War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In 1990, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> invaded Kuwait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[71]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Kuwait" title="Invasion of Kuwait">invasion of Kuwait</a> by Iraqi forces led to the 1990&#8211;91 <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>. <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> joined a multinational <a href="/wiki/Coalition_of_the_Gulf_War" title="Coalition of the Gulf War">coalition</a> that opposed Iraq. Displays of support for Iraq by <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a> and <a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">Palestine</a> resulted in strained relations between many of the Arab states. After the war, a so-called "Damascus Declaration" formalized an alliance for future joint Arab defensive actions between Egypt, Syria, and the GCC member states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[72]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport_and_industry">Transport and industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabian_Peninsula&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Transport and industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The extraction and refining of oil and gas are the major industrial activities in the Arabian Peninsula. The region also has an active construction sector, with many cities reflecting the wealth generated by the oil industry. The service sector is dominated by financial and technical institutions, which, like the construction sector, mainly serve the oil industry. Traditional handicrafts such as carpet-weaving are found in rural areas of Arabia.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallery">Gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabian_Peninsula&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Gallery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sanaa.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1818" data-file-width="1228" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Sanaa.JPG/81px-Sanaa.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Sanaa.JPG/121px-Sanaa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Sanaa.JPG/162px-Sanaa.JPG 2x" width="81"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The old city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sana%27a" title="Sana\'a">Sana\'a</a>, Yemen. Peninsular Arabs trace their lineage to <a href="/wiki/Qahtanite" title="Qahtanite">Qahtan</a>, who was reportedly based in Yemen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laughlin2008_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-Laughlin2008-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div Hadawi\'s, Palestine Diary, pp. 30&#8211;31, the \'Damascus Protocol\' stated: "The recognition by Great Britain of the independence of the Arab countries lying within the following frontiers: North: The Line Mersin_Adana to parallel 37N. and thence along the line Birejek-Urga-Mardin-Kidiat-Jazirat (Ibn \'Unear)-Amadia to the Persian frontier; East: The Persian frontier down to the Persian Gulf; South: The Indian Ocean (with the exclusion of Aden, whose status was to be maintained). West: The Red Sea and the Mediterranean Sea back to Mersin. The abolition of all exceptional privileges granted to foreigners under the capitulations. The conclusion of a defensive alliance between Great Britain <i>and the future independent Arab State</i>. The grant of economic preference to Great Britain." see <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=n706ShSYt-sC&amp;printsec=toc&amp;dq=&amp;source=gbs_summary_s&amp;cad=0#PPA65,M1" rel="nofollow"><i>King Husain and the Kingdom of Hejaz</i></a>, By Randall Baker, Oleander Press, 1979, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-900891-48-3" title="Special:BookSources/0-900891-48-3">0-900891-48-3</a>, pages 64&#8211;65</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Salibi, Kamal Suleiman (1988). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=4vOJ15vTZV4C&amp;pg=PA60" rel="nofollow"><i>A House of Many Mansions: The History of Lebanon Reconsidered,</i></a>.
